Two Interpretations of the Threat in Q 4:93
Juwayni presents two exegetical ways to limit the application of Q 4:93.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Juwayni defends the possibility of forgiveness for a killer by reinterpreting the apparent threat in Q 4:93 through two scholarly approaches.

Core Idea
Qur'anic threats of eternal punishment can be reconciled with divine mercy through careful interpretation.

Juwayni addresses a key sectarian dispute: does Q 4:93 condemn every intentional killer to eternal Hell? He presents two interpretive strategies from classical scholarship that restrict the verse's application, showing that the threat is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two distinct interpretations Juwayni presents for Q 4:93.
The first is from Ibn Abbas, the second is based on the meaning of 'khālidan'.
2
Understand the first interpretation from Ibn Abbas
Ibn Abbas interprets 'whoever kills a believer intentionally' as referring only to those who consider killing lawful (mustahill). Juwayni provides evidence: true intent requires no deterrent, and other verses treat the killer as a brother and encourage pardon.
3
Examine the second interpretation regarding 'khālidan'
Juwayni suggests that 'abiding forever' can mean long but finite periods, as in supplications for kings. This allows the threat to be reconciled with mercy.
The term does not necessarily imply eternal duration.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these interpretations protect the possibility of forgiveness for grave sinners, countering the Khawarij and Mu'tazila who took the verse literally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تمسكوا بأي من القرآن، فمن أظهرها عندهم، قوله تبارك وتعالى: «ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا فجزاؤه جهنم خالدا فيها» (النساء: 93). وعلى ظاهر هذه الآية وجوه من الكلام، ونحن نأثر منها وجهين. أحدهما: ما روي عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أنه قال: معناه: ومن يقتل مؤمنا مستحلا قتله. ويشهد لذلك: أن العمد إنما يتمحض من يقدم على الشيء إقداما لا يعتقد يزعه عنه وازع. ومن أن القتل من أكبر الكبائر، فقد يدعوه إليه هواه، ويزعه إيمانه عنه فيقدم رجلا مشفقا، والعامد حقا هو الذي لا وازع له في رأيه. والدليل عليه: أنه تبارك وتعالى ذكر في آيات القصاص أحكامه، وصدره بقلب الإيمان، وأثبت للقاتل اسم الأخ، آخذا من أخوة الإيمان، وندب إلى العفو عنه، ولم يتعرض للوعيد، ولم يذكر في آية الوعيد حكم القصاص البتة. فهذا وجه. والثاني: قوله تبارك وتعالى: «خالدا فيها» ظاهر في التأييد، ولا يبعد حمله على الآماد الطوال وإن كانت تنتهي. وقد تجري في مخاطبة الملوك وتحياتهم الدعاء بالخلود إذ يقول القائل: خلد الله ملك الملك، ولو عنوا به تأييدا لزجروا عن سؤال المحال. والنص القاطع في وعد الله تبارك وتعالى: التجاوز عن المذنبين، وهو قوله تبارك وتعالى: «إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء» (النساء: 116). ولم يرد تعالى أنه يغفر لمن تاب، فإنه ولو أراد ذلك لما انتظم الفرق بين الشرك وغيره. والشرك مغفور له إذا تاب. فإذن من مات من عصاة أهل الإيمان من غير توبة، فأمره مغيب: إن شاء الله غفر له، أو شفع فيه شفيع، وإن شاء عرضه على النار بقدر ذنبه، ثم عاقبته الفوز الأكبر والنجاة. قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم: «لا يبقى في النار من قلبه مثقال ذرة من الإيمان».
And they have clung to some verses of the Qur’an, and among the most apparent to them is His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever' (Al-Nisa’: 93). Concerning the literal sense of this verse, there are aspects of discussion, and we will relate two aspects from them. The first: what was narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: Its meaning is: whoever kills a believer considering his killing lawful. And evidence for that: that intentionality is only realized from one who proceeds to a thing with a proceeding not believing that a deterrent will deter him. And since killing is among the major sins, his desire may call him to it, but his faith deters him, so he advances with apprehensive feet, and the truly intentional is the one who has no deterrent in his view. And the evidence for it: that He, Blessed and Exalted, mentioned in the verses of retaliation its rulings, and prefaced it with [mention of] the heart of faith, and established for the killer the name of brother, derived from brotherhood of faith, and encouraged pardon for him, and did not address the threat, and did not mention in the verse of threat the ruling of retaliation at all. So this is one aspect. And the second: His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'abiding therein forever' is apparent in perpetuity, and it is not far-fetched to carry it upon lengthy periods even if they end. And it may occur in addressing kings and their greetings the supplication for perpetuity when the speaker says: May Allah perpetuate the king’s kingdom, and if they meant by it eternity, they would be prevented from asking for the impossible. And the decisive text concerning Allah’s promise, Blessed and Exalted, is pardon for the sinners, and that is His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (Al-Nisa’: 116). And He, Exalted, did not mean that He forgives for one who repents, for if He meant that, the distinction between shirk and other sins would not be established. And shirk is forgiven if one repents. So whoever dies from among the disobedient of the people of faith without repentance, his affair is concealed: if Allah wills, He forgives him, or an intercessor intercedes for him, and if He wills, He exposes him to the Fire according to his sin, then his final outcome is the great success and salvation. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and grant peace, said: 'There will not remain in the Fire anyone in whose heart is a mustard seed’s weight of faith.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the possibility of forgiveness for grave sinners (murtakib al-kabira) from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama‘ah, against the Khawarij and Mu‘tazila who interpreted the Qur’anic threats absolutely. Juwayni presents two ways of interpreting the apparent meaning of Q 4:93, which threatens eternal punishment for killing a believer. The first interpretation, attributed to Ibn Abbas, restricts the threat to those who consider killing lawful (mustahill), not to every intentional killer. The evidence: true intent (al-‘amd) is only present when no deterrent exists; a believer who kills despite his faith is internally conflicted (rajulan mushfiqan), not fully purposed. Moreover, verses about retaliation (Q 2:178) refer to the killer as a brother, encourage pardon, and mention no eternal threat. Thus, the apparent threat is not universally applicable. The second interpretation: the word 'khālidan fīhā' (abiding forever) can denote long periods in Arabic usage, as in supplications for kings ('khallada Allahu mulka al-malik'), which do not imply actual eternity. Furthermore, the decisive text is Q 4:116, which promises forgiveness for all sins except shirk if Allah wills. Juwayni argues that if Q 4:93 were absolute, it would contradict Q 4:116. Therefore, the fate of a sinner who dies without repentance is unresolved (maghib): he is under Allah’s will — either forgiven through His mercy or intercession, or punished temporarily and then saved. The hadith confirms that no one with faith equal to a mustard seed will remain eternally in Hell. Thus, the lesson affirms the doctrine of the possibility of eventual salvation for all monotheists.

Key Terms
مستحل
العمد
الوعيد
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Q 4:93 proves that every intentional killer of a believer will be eternally damned.
Correction: Juwayni shows the verse is interpreted restrictively: either limited to those who deem killing lawful, or the term 'khālidan' can mean long but finite duration, allowing for eventual mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Juwayni's first interpretation of Q 4:93, to whom does the threat of eternal punishment apply?
A) Every intentional killer of a believer
B) Only those who kill a believer considering it lawful
C) Only those who kill a polytheist
D) Those who kill after committing shirk

Key Takeaway
Juwayni provides two scholarly interpretations that prevent Q 4:93 from contradicting Allah's mercy for monotheists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin you know is grave, do you truly tremble at the threat of Allah's punishment, or do you secretly assume mercy will cover you without wrestling with the Qur'an's warnings? How does Juwayni's careful handling of this verse challenge you to take both the threat and mercy seriously in your own heart?
?
Are there sins in your life that you have normalized or made excuses for—perhaps by telling yourself 'I'm not really that bad' or 'I don't intend to disobey'? How does the concept of 'mustahill' (considering something lawful) force you to examine whether you have unconsciously justified what Allah has forbidden?
?
Have you ever dismissed a verse of seeming unconditionally punishing—like Q 4:93—without digging into how scholars like Juwayni actually interpreted it? What would it cost you to personally wrestle with such verses, rather than avoid them, and let them shape your hope in Allah's mercy and fear of His justice?

The Fate of Grave Sinners: Between Threat and Mercy
Juwayni establishes that grave sinners may be forgiven or eventually saved, using Q 4:116 and a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the ultimate fate of grave sinners who die without repentance, based on Q 4:116 and the hadith about a mustard seed of faith.

Core Idea
No believer with any faith will remain in Hell forever; ultimate salvation is assured.

After addressing the apparent threat, Juwayni turns to the decisive Qur'anic promise that Allah forgives all sins other than shirk as He wills, and cites a hadith confirming that even the smallest amount of faith guarantees eventual exit from Hell.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the decisive text Q 4:116
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والنص القاطع' and its translation, paying attention to the argument.
This verse is key: it promises forgiveness for all sins except shirk.
2
Understand Juwayni's argument
Juwayni argues that the verse does not require repentance before forgiveness, because if it did, the distinction between shirk and other sins would collapse. Shirk is also forgiven upon repentance.
Thus, it applies to those who die unrepentant.
3
Consider the three possibilities
For a believer who dies in sin without repentance, his affair is concealed: either Allah forgives him, intercession is accepted, or he is punished temporarily and then enters Paradise.
All outcomes lead to eventual salvation.
4
Note the hadith
The Prophet said: 'No one with faith equal to a mustard seed will remain in the Fire.' This confirms that no believer will be eternally punished.
This is a fundamental Ash'ari doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تمسكوا بأي من القرآن، فمن أظهرها عندهم، قوله تبارك وتعالى: «ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا فجزاؤه جهنم خالدا فيها» (النساء: 93). وعلى ظاهر هذه الآية وجوه من الكلام، ونحن نأثر منها وجهين. أحدهما: ما روي عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أنه قال: معناه: ومن يقتل مؤمنا مستحلا قتله. ويشهد لذلك: أن العمد إنما يتمحض من يقدم على الشيء إقداما لا يعتقد يزعه عنه وازع. ومن أن القتل من أكبر الكبائر، فقد يدعوه إليه هواه، ويزعه إيمانه عنه فيقدم رجلا مشفقا، والعامد حقا هو الذي لا وازع له في رأيه. والدليل عليه: أنه تبارك وتعالى ذكر في آيات القصاص أحكامه، وصدره بقلب الإيمان، وأثبت للقاتل اسم الأخ، آخذا من أخوة الإيمان، وندب إلى العفو عنه، ولم يتعرض للوعيد، ولم يذكر في آية الوعيد حكم القصاص البتة. فهذا وجه. والثاني: قوله تبارك وتعالى: «خالدا فيها» ظاهر في التأييد، ولا يبعد حمله على الآماد الطوال وإن كانت تنتهي. وقد تجري في مخاطبة الملوك وتحياتهم الدعاء بالخلود إذ يقول القائل: خلد الله ملك الملك، ولو عنوا به تأييدا لزجروا عن سؤال المحال. والنص القاطع في وعد الله تبارك وتعالى: التجاوز عن المذنبين، وهو قوله تبارك وتعالى: «إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء» (النساء: 116). ولم يرد تعالى أنه يغفر لمن تاب، فإنه ولو أراد ذلك لما انتظم الفرق بين الشرك وغيره. والشرك مغفور له إذا تاب. فإذن من مات من عصاة أهل الإيمان من غير توبة، فأمره مغيب: إن شاء الله غفر له، أو شفع فيه شفيع، وإن شاء عرضه على النار بقدر ذنبه، ثم عاقبته الفوز الأكبر والنجاة. قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم: «لا يبقى في النار من قلبه مثقال ذرة من الإيمان».
And they have clung to some verses of the Qur’an, and among the most apparent to them is His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever' (Al-Nisa’: 93). Concerning the literal sense of this verse, there are aspects of discussion, and we will relate two aspects from them. The first: what was narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: Its meaning is: whoever kills a believer considering his killing lawful. And evidence for that: that intentionality is only realized from one who proceeds to a thing with a proceeding not believing that a deterrent will deter him. And since killing is among the major sins, his desire may call him to it, but his faith deters him, so he advances with apprehensive feet, and the truly intentional is the one who has no deterrent in his view. And the evidence for it: that He, Blessed and Exalted, mentioned in the verses of retaliation its rulings, and prefaced it with [mention of] the heart of faith, and established for the killer the name of brother, derived from brotherhood of faith, and encouraged pardon for him, and did not address the threat, and did not mention in the verse of threat the ruling of retaliation at all. So this is one aspect. And the second: His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'abiding therein forever' is apparent in perpetuity, and it is not far-fetched to carry it upon lengthy periods even if they end. And it may occur in addressing kings and their greetings the supplication for perpetuity when the speaker says: May Allah perpetuate the king’s kingdom, and if they meant by it eternity, they would be prevented from asking for the impossible. And the decisive text concerning Allah’s promise, Blessed and Exalted, is pardon for the sinners, and that is His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (Al-Nisa’: 116). And He, Exalted, did not mean that He forgives for one who repents, for if He meant that, the distinction between shirk and other sins would not be established. And shirk is forgiven if one repents. So whoever dies from among the disobedient of the people of faith without repentance, his affair is concealed: if Allah wills, He forgives him, or an intercessor intercedes for him, and if He wills, He exposes him to the Fire according to his sin, then his final outcome is the great success and salvation. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and grant peace, said: 'There will not remain in the Fire anyone in whose heart is a mustard seed’s weight of faith.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the possibility of forgiveness for grave sinners (murtakib al-kabira) from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama‘ah, against the Khawarij and Mu‘tazila who interpreted the Qur’anic threats absolutely. Juwayni presents two ways of interpreting the apparent meaning of Q 4:93, which threatens eternal punishment for killing a believer. The first interpretation, attributed to Ibn Abbas, restricts the threat to those who consider killing lawful (mustahill), not to every intentional killer. The evidence: true intent (al-‘amd) is only present when no deterrent exists; a believer who kills despite his faith is internally conflicted (rajulan mushfiqan), not fully purposed. Moreover, verses about retaliation (Q 2:178) refer to the killer as a brother, encourage pardon, and mention no eternal threat. Thus, the apparent threat is not universally applicable. The second interpretation: the word 'khālidan fīhā' (abiding forever) can denote long periods in Arabic usage, as in supplications for kings ('khallada Allahu mulka al-malik'), which do not imply actual eternity. Furthermore, the decisive text is Q 4:116, which promises forgiveness for all sins except shirk if Allah wills. Juwayni argues that if Q 4:93 were absolute, it would contradict Q 4:116. Therefore, the fate of a sinner who dies without repentance is unresolved (maghib): he is under Allah’s will — either forgiven through His mercy or intercession, or punished temporarily and then saved. The hadith confirms that no one with faith equal to a mustard seed will remain eternally in Hell. Thus, the lesson affirms the doctrine of the possibility of eventual salvation for all monotheists.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
أهل الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Grave sinners who die without repentance will be eternally punished in Hell.
Correction: Juwayni shows they may be forgiven, interceded for, or punished temporarily then saved; no believer remains forever.

Checkpoint
According to Juwayni, what is the fate of a believer who dies unrepentant after committing a major sin?
A) Eternal Hell
B) Automatically forgiven
C) His affair is concealed: either forgiveness, intercession, or temporary punishment then paradise
D) He is subject to the will of God only

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris believe that no believer, regardless of sin, will remain in Hell permanently; the Qur'an and hadith assure eventual mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to face Allah tonight with a major sin unrepented for, would your hope rest on His mercy, intercession, or the hadith? Which of these three possibilities do you most rely on in your daily life, and why?
?
The hadith says that even a mustard seed’s weight of faith ensures eventual salvation. But does your current faith feel like it could survive the Fire if it had to? How would you strengthen it starting today, not just for safety but for love?
?
Juwayni’s argument shows that your eternal destiny is tied to the reality of your faith, not just your repentance. What is one area where your actions might contradict the faith in your heart, and will you commit to change it before tonight?

Repentance: Rationally Obligatory and Scripturally Affirmed
Juwayni states the rational obligation of God accepting repentance, citing Quran and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's claim that God's acceptance of repentance is rationally obligatory and supported by revealed texts.

Core Idea
Repentance is not just recommended; God's acceptance of it is rationally necessary and scripturally affirmed.

Juwayni begins a new section on repentance (tawbah) by making a strong claim: it is rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqlan) upon God to accept repentance. He then immediately grounds this rational obligation in scriptural proofs from the Quran and hadith. This lesson unpacks that dual foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let Juwayni's own words come through first.
Note the phrase 'واجب عقلا' – this is a key technical term.
2
Identify the evidence
Juwayni gives two pieces of scriptural evidence: a Quranic verse and a hadith. Find them and note how they support his claim.
The Quranic verse is from Surah ash-Shura (42:25). The hadith is well-known.
3
Reflect on the rational obligation
Consider why Juwayni might say acceptance of repentance is rationally obligatory. What does this imply about God's justice and mercy?
This rational aspect is not expanded here but sets the tone for the section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل يجب قبول التوبة على الله عقلا. ولكن ورد الشرع بقبولها (١) قال الله تبارك وتعالى: «وهو الذى يقبل التوبة عن عباده» (٢٥ الشورى) وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم: «التائب من الذنب كمن لا ذنب له).
Section: Acceptance of repentance is obligatory upon God rationally. But the Sharia came with its acceptance. (1) Allah, Blessed and Exalted, said: 'And He is the One who accepts repentance from His servants' (Surah ash-Shura, 25). And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The one who repents from sin is like one who has no sin.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. Juwayni begins by stating that it is rationally obligatory (واجب عقلاً) upon God to accept repentance. He then notes that the revealed law (الشرع) confirms this acceptance. He provides two pieces of evidence: a Quranic verse from Surah ash-Shura (42:25) where Allah describes Himself as accepting repentance from His servants, and a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) says that a repentant person is like one who has no sin. The rational obligation is implied by God's justice and mercy, though Juwayni does not expand here. The scriptural evidence is direct. The footnote (1) likely indicates a variant reading.

Necessary Attributes: Power, Will, Knowledge, Life
Juwayni deduces four necessary attributes from contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical sequence of necessary attributes (power, will, knowledge, life) that Juwayni deduces from the contingency of the world.

Core Idea
The world's contingency logically implies a Creator who is living, powerful, willing, and knowing.

Juwayni presents a concise yet profound deduction: from the fact that the world is originated (hadith), we can infer that its Originator (muhdith) must possess certain attributes. This passage establishes a logical chain starting from power, then will, then knowledge, culminating in life. Understanding this sequence is key to the Ash'ari understanding of God's necessary attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical connectors (فإن, إذ, وقبل أن).
Pay attention to the repetition of 'before' (قبل أن) which indicates logical priority, not temporal.
2
Trace the logical deduction
Start from the premise 'the world is originated'. Work through each step: originator must be powerful because creation needs power; powerful must be willing to choose; willing must be knowing to know what is willed; and all three presuppose life.
This is a chain: world → power → will → knowledge → life.
3
Identify the key attributes
List the four attributes mentioned: power (qadir), will (murid), knowledge ('alim), life (hayy). Note that life is the foundation that enables the others.
All four are considered necessary attributes of God in Ash'ari theology.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'before'
The word 'before' (قبل أن) does not mean time but logical priority. God's attributes are eternal, but some logically depend on others. For example, will logically presupposes knowledge.
This avoids the misconception of temporal sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث أن العالم حادث فإن محدثه يجب أن يكون قادرا، إذ لو لم يكن قادرا لما أوجد العالم. وقبل أن يكون الله قادرا يكون مريدا، وقبل أن يكون الله مريدا يكون عالما، وقبل أن يكون قادرا مريدا عالما يكون حيا.
Since the world is originated, then its originator must be powerful (qadir), for if He were not powerful He would not have brought the world into existence. And before God is powerful, He is willing (murid), and before God is willing, He is knowing ('alim), and before He is powerful, willing, knowing, He is living (hayy).
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage deduces the necessary attributes of God from the fact that the world is created (hadith). Since the world is originated, its originator must be powerful (qadir), because power is needed to create. Furthermore, a powerful agent must also be willing (murid) to choose to create, and willing requires knowledge ('alim) to know what is being created. Finally, power, will, and knowledge together presuppose life (hayy). Thus, the originator must be living, powerful, willing, and knowing. This is a standard sequence in kalam for establishing the attributes of God from the fact of creation.

Key Terms
حادث (hadith)
محدث (muhdith)
قادر (qadir)
مريد (murid)
عالم ('alim)
حي (hayy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attributes of God are created or come into existence over time.
Correction: The passage uses logical priority (قبل أن) not temporal priority. All attributes are eternal; the sequence is about conceptual dependence.

Checkpoint

A) Life
B) Will
C) Knowledge
D) Speech

Key Takeaway
The world's contingency logically entails that its Creator is living, powerful, willing, and knowing, with these attributes being eternally present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every breath you take is contingent upon a Creator who is living, powerful, willing, and knowing, are you living as if you are self-sufficient? What specific area of your life today—your work, your relationships, your planning—are you acting as if you are in control rather than acknowledging your absolute dependence on Him? Commit one concrete action this week to remind yourself of that dependence.
?
The passage says God must be willing before He acts. When you reflect on your own intentions, do you align your will with what God knows to be good? Think of a decision you are facing right now. Are you acting without truly knowing the outcome or without seeking God's guidance? How will you invite God's knowledge into that decision today?

Proof of God's Attributes from the Temporality of the World
Juwayni proves God's power, will, knowledge, and life from the temporality of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Juwayni proves that God is powerful, willing, knowing, and living based on the fact that the world is temporally originated.

Core Idea
The world's createdness proves God's power, will, knowledge, and life in a logical chain.

Imam al-Juwayni presents a concise argument that begins with a shared premise—the world is temporally originated—and then deduces four essential attributes of God. Each attribute follows necessarily from the previous one. This passage shows how sound theology builds upon rational inference from observable reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the word 'وجب' (must be) indicating necessity.
2
Identify the logical sequence
The passage presents four attributes in a specific order: power, will, knowledge, life. Notice how each attribute is introduced with 'وقبل أن' (and before that).
The sequence is: qadir → murid → alim → hayy.
3
Understand why each attribute is necessary
For each step, ask: why must a powerful creator also be willing? Why must a willing creator also be knowing? Why do these three require life? The commentary explains the reasoning.
Each prior attribute logically presupposes the next.
4
Reflect on the misconception
A common misunderstanding is that God acquires these attributes over time. Reflect on why the sequence is logical, not temporal. God has all attributes eternally.
The word 'before' (قبل) here means logical priority, not time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحدث في صفات الله تعالى . فقال : حيث أن العالم حادث فان محدثه يجب أن يكون قادرا . اذ لو لم يكن قادرا لما أوجد العالم . وقبل أن يكون الله قادرا يكون مريدا وقبل أن يكون الله مريدا يكون عالما. وقبل أن يكون قادرا مريدا عالما يكون حيا .
And he spoke about the attributes of Allah the Exalted. He said: Since the world is temporally originated, its originator must be powerful. For if he were not powerful, he would not have brought the world into existence. And before God is powerful, He must be willing; and before God is willing, He must be knowing; and before He is powerful, willing, knowing, He must be living.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a sequential proof of four essential attributes of God. The argument begins with the premise that the world is temporally originated (hadith). From this, it deduces that its originator must be powerful (qadir), since producing a temporally originated entity requires power. Next, it argues that power presupposes will (irada), because a powerful agent acts according to choice. Will in turn presupposes knowledge (ilm), as one cannot will something without knowing it. Finally, power, will, and knowledge together presuppose life (hayat), since only a living agent can possess these attributes. The proof is linear: temporality → power → will → knowledge → life. Each step is necessary for the previous to be meaningful. This establishes that God is living, knowing, willing, and powerful.

Key Terms
حادث (hadith)
قادر (qadir)
مريد (murid)
عالم (alim)
حي (hayy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sequence suggests God acquires power first, then will, then knowledge, then life over time.
Correction: The sequence is logical, not temporal. God possesses all attributes eternally; the order is for understanding, not chronology.

Checkpoint

A) Because He is eternal
B) Because the world is temporally originated
C) Because He is living
D) Because He is knowing

Key Takeaway
From the temporality of the world, we rationally deduce that its Creator is living, knowing, willing, and powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The proof concludes that God is living. Since He is alive and fully aware of every moment, how does that reality confront you in your private thoughts or actions that no one else sees? Will you remind yourself of this when you are alone?

Juwayni's View on Divine Attributes: Identity with Essence
Juwayni presents his stance that attributes are identical to essence, contrasting with al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's position that God's attributes are identical to His essence and how it differs from the view of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.

Core Idea
For Juwayni, God's attributes are not separate; they are His essence itself.

This lesson examines Juwayni's stance in the classical debate on divine attributes. While Muslims agree that God has attributes, they differ on whether these attributes are identical to or additional to the essence. Juwayni argues for identity, meaning that God's knowledge is simply His being knowing, not an added entity.

Guided Path
1
Read Juwayni's key claim
Read the passage carefully and note Juwayni's explicit statement that God's attributes are identical to His essence (ʿayn al-dhāt).
Juwayni uses the phrase 'la maʿnā li-l-ʿilmi illā kawnu l-ʿālimi ʿāliman' to illustrate.
2
Contrast with al-Razi
Identify how Juwayni contrasts his view with that of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who holds that attributes are additional to the essence (zāʾidah ʿalā al-dhāt) and necessary through another.
Note the terms 'wājibāt bi-l-ghayr' and 'mumkināt fī ḥaddi dhātihā'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how Juwayni's position avoids multiplicity in the divine essence while still affirming real attributes.
This is a key point of difference from the Ashʿarī position held by al-Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : لا جدال فى هذا بين كل من انتمى الى الاسلام . ولكن الجدال في ما معناه : هل صفات الله هي عين ذاته . أم صفاته زائدة على الذات غير منفكة عنها ؟ ويرى أن صفات الله هي عين ذاته يقول : لا معنى للعلم الا كون العالم عالما » - « وكونه مريدا عين ارادته » خلافا لفخر الدين الرازى الذى جاء في كلامه : ان الصفات زائدات على الذات ، واجبات بالغير ممكنات في حد ذاتها .
Then he said: There is no dispute about this among all who belong to Islam. But the dispute is about its meaning: Are God's attributes identical to His essence, or are His attributes additional to the essence, inseparable from it? And he holds that God's attributes are identical to His essence. He says: 'There is no meaning to knowledge except that the knower is knowing' and 'His being willing is exactly His will', contrary to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who stated in his speech that attributes are additional to the essence, necessary through another, and possible in themselves.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage introduces the classical theological debate on the nature of God's attributes. Juwayni first states that there is consensus among Muslims on the fact that God possesses attributes (no dispute about that). The disagreement is over whether the attributes are identical to God's essence (ayn al-dhat) or are additional to it (za'idah 'ala al-dhat) while being inseparable. Juwayni explicitly adopts the first position: the attributes are identical to the essence. For example, God's knowledge is nothing other than His being knowing; His being willing is exactly His will. He contrasts this view with that of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who held that attributes are additional entities that are necessary through God (wajibat bi'l-ghayr) but possible in themselves. This sets the stage for the subsequent arguments and refutations.

Key Terms
عين الذات (ayn al-dhat)
زائدة على الذات (za'idah 'ala al-dhat)
غير منفكة (ghayr munfakkah)
واجبات بالغير (wajibat bi'l-ghayr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Juwayni's view that attributes are identical to essence means attributes are not real.
Correction: Juwayni affirms real attributes; his point is that they are not separate entities added to the essence, but rather the essence itself qualified by them.

Checkpoint
What is Juwayni's position on the relationship between God's attributes and His essence?
A) Attributes are additional but inseparable
B) Attributes are identical to the essence
C) Attributes are separate from the essence
D) Attributes are created

Key Takeaway
Juwayni asserts that God's attributes are identical to His essence, avoiding multiplicity while preserving real attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are identical to His essence, then every attribute reveals Him directly. Which attribute of God have you most neglected in your daily awareness, and what specific action will you take this week to make it a living reality in your heart?
?
Juwayni says knowledge is nothing other than the knower being knowing. This means God is not just knowing but is knowledge itself. When you sin, do you truly act as if God's knowledge is identical to His being—that He knows you completely and yet you hide? What will you confess to Him today that you have been hiding?
?
Al-Razi thought attributes are additional, making them possible in themselves. Could it be that you sometimes treat God's attributes as optional extras rather than His very essence—for example, thinking His mercy is something He 'has' rather than what He 'is'? How would your life change if you believed God is mercy itself?

Juwayni's Refutation of Ash'ari Argument for Additional Attributes
Juwayni presents and refutes the Ash'ari argument for additional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument for additional attributes and Juwayni's refutation using the human analogy.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are real but not separate entities; they are the essence itself.

The Ash'aris argued that if attributes were identical to the essence, then knowledge and power would be one. Juwayni counters with a powerful analogy from common human experience, showing that distinct attributes can coexist in a single essence without multiplicity. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Ash'ari claim, their proof, then Juwayni's response.
The language is classical kalam; take your time.
2
Identify the Ash'ari proof
The Ash'aris argue that if knowledge and power were identical to the essence, they would be identical to each other. Find that logical step in the text.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze Juwayni's refutation
Juwayni uses the example of one person who knows, wills, and is powerful. Notice how he shows that these attributes appear independently yet are inseparable from the person.
The analogy is meant to show that distinct attributes do not require separate essences.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Juwayni's point is that attributes are real but not additional entities; they are the essence itself in relation to different actions.
This aligns with the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأشاعرة يقولون : ان الله صفات زائدة فهو عالم يعلم ، قادر بقدرة مريد بارادة . وأقوى دليل لهم قولهم : لو كان العلم نفس الذات ، والقدرة نفس الذات . لكان العلم نفس القدرة ، فكان المفهوم من العلم والقدرة واحدا . ونقول لهم : انا نرى الشخص الواحد يعلم ويريد ويقدر . ويظهر علمه للناس باستقلال ، وتظهر ارادته باستقلال ، وتظهر قدرته باستقلال . وعلمه وارادته وقدرته كامنة في شخصه غير منفكة عنه بحيث لو رآه شخص آخر مقابلة لم يحكم بالعدد بين الشخص وصفاته ، بل يحكم برؤية شخص واحد . وكلما تظهر أثر صفة حكم له بها دون ما قول بالفصل بين الذات والصفة
And the Ash'aris say: God has additional attributes, so He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, willing by will. And their strongest proof is their statement: If knowledge were the essence itself, and power were the essence itself, then knowledge would be power, and the concept of knowledge and power would be one. And we say to them: We see one person knows, wills, and is powerful. His knowledge appears to people independently, His will appears independently, and His power appears independently. Yet his knowledge, will, and power are latent in his person, inseparable from him, such that if another person sees him face to face, he does not judge multiplicity between the person and his attributes, but judges that he sees one person. Whenever an effect of an attribute appears, one judges him by it, without saying separation between essence and attribute.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari argument for attributes being additional to the essence, followed by Juwayni's refutation. The Ash'aris argue that if knowledge and power were identical to the divine essence, then knowledge would be identical to power, merging their concepts – but we know they are distinct. Therefore, they must be additional. Juwayni responds by drawing an analogy from human experience. When we see a person who knows, wills, and has power, we observe that these attributes appear independently in their effects, yet we do not posit that the person has multiple separate parts or that the essence is separate from the attributes. Rather, we regard the person as a single entity who is knowing, willing, and powerful. The attributes are latent in the person and inseparable. Similarly for God, we affirm distinct attributes without dividing the essence. The key is that the attributes are not separate entities, but the essence itself has these properties, even though conceptually distinct.

Key Terms
علم (ilm)
قدرة (qudra)
إرادة (irada)
زيادة الصفات (ziyadat al-sifat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Juwayni denies the reality of divine attributes.
Correction: Juwayni affirms real attributes but argues they are not separate entities from the essence; they are the essence itself in different respects.

Checkpoint

A) Attributes are created
B) If attributes are identical to essence, then knowledge would be power
C) God cannot have attributes
D) Attributes are separable from essence

Key Takeaway
Juwayni refutes the Ash'ari argument for additional attributes by showing that distinct attributes can inhere in a single essence without multiplicity, as seen in human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's power and knowledge, do you ever unconsciously treat them as separate 'powers' that exist alongside His being? How might that affect your feeling of closeness to Him, knowing that these attributes are not separate from His essence but are Him?
?
The Ash'ari argument looked logical, but Juwayni showed that distinct concepts do not require separate existence. Where in your own understanding of God might you be making similar logical leaps—areas where you assume separation where there is unity?
?
If God's attributes are not additional but inherent, does that change the way you call upon Him? For example, when you ask for His mercy, do you see mercy as an extra thing He gives, or as an expression of who He essentially is? How would your trust shift if you truly internalized that His mercy is not a separate attribute but His very self acting?

The Mu'tazili View on Attributes of Essence
Juwayni presents the Mu'tazili position on attributes and their argument against separate attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili position that God's attributes are identical with His essence, and their argument that separate attributes would imply multiple eternal beings.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, attributes are the essence itself, not separate entities.

Imam Juwayni presents the Mu'tazili perspective on divine attributes, explaining their insistence that attributes are inherent in God's essence and not additional. This careful reasoning aims to preserve the oneness of God by avoiding any implication of multiple eternal beings. The passage sets the stage for understanding a key theological debate in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazili claim, then their reasoning.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'fi al-dhat' and 'qadim'.
2
Identify the core claim
The Mu'tazila say attributes are 'fi al-dhat' – within the essence. What does this mean in contrast to the view that attributes are additional?
3
Follow the argument step-by-step
Trace the logical steps: God is eternal; attribute is eternal; if separate, two eternals; therefore, attributes must be one with essence.
Consider why this reasoning is important for tawhid.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this view upholds tawhid but also shapes the understanding of God's attributes. Juwayni notes its alignment with his own stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة - يرحمهم الله يقولون ان صفات الله فى ذات الله ، - وهو هو بذاته وصفاته من قبل وجود العالم ومن بعده والى ما لا نهاية . ولم يسرحوا بصفات زائدة على ذات الله . لأن الله قديم ، والصفة : قديمة . فيلزم فى العالم قديمان : الله والصفة اذا قيل بانفصالها . أما اذا قيل بالصفة فى الذات وهى والذات شيء واحد فانه يلزم في العالم : قديم واحد موصوف بكل كمال وهو ما يجب القول به
And the Mu'tazila – may God have mercy on them – say that God's attributes are in God's essence, and He is the same in His essence and attributes before the existence of the world and after it and unto infinity. And they did not affirm attributes additional to God's essence, because God is eternal, and the attribute is eternal, so there would follow in the world two eternals: God and the attribute, if one says they are separate. But if one says the attribute is in the essence and it and the essence are one thing, then there follows in the world one eternal described with every perfection, and that is what must be said.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazili position on the attributes. The Mu'tazila affirm that God's attributes are inherent in His essence (fi dhat Allah) and not additional entities. They argue that if attributes were separate from the essence (or considered as distinct realities), then there would be multiple eternal beings (qadimayn), which would compromise tawhid. Since God is eternal, and a separate attribute would also be eternal, that would necessitate two eternals. Therefore, they hold that the attributes are identical to the essence – the essence itself is described by every perfection, and there is only one eternal being. Juwayni presents this view without explicit rejection, though he himself holds a similar position. Note that Juwayni's own view (attributes identical to essence) aligns with the Mu'tazili view presented here, but he differs in that he still affirms real attributes without separation.

Key Terms
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
قديم (qadim)
قديمان (qadiman)
في الذات (fi al-dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that God has any attributes at all.
Correction: They affirm attributes but as identical with the essence, not as separate entities. They affirm that God is knowing, powerful, etc., but without multiplicity of eternal beings.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila reject additional attributes according to this passage?
A) Because attributes are created
B) Because it would imply two eternal beings
C) Because God cannot have attributes
D) Because attributes are the essence itself

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila maintained that God's attributes are not additional to His essence, for that would imply two eternal beings; rather, attributes are identical with the essence, ensuring the oneness of the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you sometimes mentally separate them from His essence, treating knowledge as an 'object' He possesses? This lesson shows the danger of inadvertently multiplying the Eternal. Examine your own heart: is there any association in your mind where you treat God's attributes as though they are alongside Him rather than being Him? Will you repent from such subtle shirk and affirm that He is knowing by His essence?
?
The Mu'tazila were so protective of tawhid they argued attributes are identical with the essence. But does your theological correctness keep you from actually experiencing His attributes? Have you reduced His knowledge to a concept, while forgetting that He knows you intimately right now? Today, let one of His attributes—like His seeing or His power—break into your daily actions. Will you live this day knowing He sees every move?
?
This passage reveals the Mu'tazila's desire to avoid two eternals. But what about you? What 'eternal' things do you cling to besides God—your wealth, reputation, relationships—as though they will last? These are the 'two eternals' of your heart. Will you, right now, identify one such attachment and consciously declare that only God is eternal, and submit to the reality that everything else will perish?

Juwayni's Proof for the Eternity of Divine Speech
Juwayni uses an analogy to prove eternal divine speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's argument that God's speech is eternal by analogy with human inner speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is as ancient as His essence, not created at revelation.

This lesson explores a concise but powerful analogy from Imam al-Juwayni. He uses a common human experience—planning a conversation—to demonstrate that meaningful speech exists in the soul before it is uttered. This analogy serves as a key proof in the Ash'ari school that divine speech is an eternal attribute of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the analogy Juwayni presents.
2
Understand the analogy
Juwayni says a person who resolves to speak with a companion after a month already has the meanings in his soul. Why does he compare this to God's speech?
Think about what 'meanings in the soul' means for God.
3
Connect to the doctrine of divine speech
This analogy shows that meaningful speech pre-exists in the speaker's essence. For God, this means His speech is eternal, not brought into being at revelation.
Distinguish between eternal inner speech (kalam nafsi) and temporal uttered speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكد الجويني على مذهبه في الصفات وهو يتحدث في كلام الله تعالى فقال : ( إن من يعزم على مفاوضة صاحب له بعد شهر ، فالمعاني التي سيوردها عند جريان الجواز يجدها بأعيانها قائمة في نفسه
And Juwayni emphasized his doctrine on attributes while speaking about the speech of Allah the Exalted. He said: 'Indeed, whoever resolves to converse with his companion after a month will find the very meanings that he will produce at the time of discourse already present in his soul.'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents Juwayni's argument for the pre-existent and eternal nature of God's speech (kalam). He uses an analogy: a person who determines to have a conversation with a friend a month from now already has in his mind the very meanings he intends to express. These meanings exist in his soul (nafs) before they are uttered. Similarly, God's speech is eternal and subsisting in His essence; it does not come into being at the moment of revelation. This is a standard Ash'ari-Juwayni argument to support that the Quran is uncreated and that God's attribute of speech is eternal, without implying that the sounds and letters are eternal. The passage is brief but encapsulates a key proof for the eternal nature of divine speech.

Key Terms
كلام الله
مفاوضة
معاني
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran (Arabic text) is eternal in its letters and sounds.
Correction: Juwayni does not claim that the actual Arabic letters are eternal; rather, the meaningful speech (kalam nafsi) is eternal. The uttered words are created expressions of the eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
What does Juwayni's analogy of a person's intention to speak after a month illustrate?
A) That speech is created at the moment of utterance
B) That meanings can exist in the soul before utterance
C) That God's speech is temporal
D) That human speech is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's speech is eternal because the meanings reside in His essence, just as intended meanings exist in the soul before utterance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you experience it as if God is speaking directly to you now, or do you treat it as a historical text? How would viewing it as the eternal speech of God—always present in His essence—change your attentiveness?
?
Think of a time you carefully prepared what you would say to someone. The words you eventually spoke were just an expression of meanings already in your mind. Does Juwayni's analogy help you trust that God's words in the Quran are perfectly intended and wise, not accidental? How does that affect your submission to them?
?
If God's speech is an eternal attribute of His being, then the Quran is not just a book but part of who He is. In what practical ways can you align your life daily with the reality that you are responding to God's eternal word, not just human prose?

The Ash'ari View: Attributes as Additional Yet Inseparable
Juwayni outlines the Ash'ari belief in additional attributes and their logical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God's attributes are additional to His essence yet inseparable, and their logical proof against the identity view.

Core Idea
Ash'aris argue attributes are real and distinct from essence, yet not separate entities.

Juwayni now turns to the dominant Sunni position: the Ash'ari school teaches that God has attributes (knowledge, power, will) that are real and additional to His essence, but not external or separable. The strongest proof is a logical reductio: if attributes were identical to essence, they would collapse into each other. The passage also defends against an objection based on the unity of the person.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the Ash'ari position and their strongest proof. Pay attention to the logical dilemma.
The proof aims to show that identifying attributes with essence leads to absurdity.
2
Analyze the proof structure
The proof says: if knowledge and power are both identical to essence, then they are identical to each other. But knowledge and power are distinct concepts. Therefore, they cannot be identical to essence.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Examine the reply to the objection
The Ash'ari then answers an objection: doesn't a single person have multiple attributes without separation? The reply is that attributes are latent in the person, not separate entities, and a viewer sees one person. Similarly, God's attributes are in His essence.
This rebuts the charge of multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فى هذا بين كل من انتمى الى الاسلام . ولكن الجدال في ما معناه : هل صفات الله هي عين ذاته . أم صفاته زائدة على الذات غير منفكة عنها ؟ ويرى أن صفات الله هى عين ذاته يقول : لا معنى للعلم الا كون العالم عالما » - « وكونه مريدا عين ارادته » خلافا لفخر الدين الرازى الذى جاء في كلامه : أن الصفات زائدات على الذات ، واجبات بالغير ممكنات في حد ذاتها . والأشاعرة يقولون : ان الله صفات زائدة فهو عالم يعلم ، قادر بقدرة مريد بارادة . وأقوى دليل لهم قولهم : لو كان العلم نفس الذات ، والقدرة نفس الذات . لكان العلم نفس القدرة ، فكان المفهوم من العلم والقدرة واحدا . ونقول لهم : انا نرى الشخص الواحد يعلم ويريد ويقدر . ويظهر علمه للناس باستقلال ، وتظهر ارادته باستقلال ، وتظهر قدرته باستقلال . وعلمه وارادته وقدرته كامنة في شخصه غير منفكة عنه بحيث لو رآه شخص آخر مقابلة لم يحكم بالعدد بين الشخص وصفاته ، بل يحكم برؤية شخص واحد . وكلما تظهر أثر صفة حكم له بها دون ما قول بالفصل بين الذات والصفة . والمعتزلة - يرحمهم الله يقولون ان صفات الله فى ذات الله ، - وهو هو بذاته وصفاته من قبل وجود العالم ومن بعده والى ما لا نهاية . ولم يسرحوا بصفات زائدة على ذات الله . لأن الله قديم ، والصفة : قديمة . فيلزم فى العالم قديمان : الله والصفة اذا قيل بانفصالها . أما اذا قيل بالصفة فى الذات وهى والذات شيء واحد فانه يلزم في العالم : قديم واحد موصوف بكل كمال وهو ما يجب القول به
In this [work] is an explanation for everyone who belongs to Islam. But the debate is about its meaning: Are God's attributes identical to His essence? Or are His attributes additional to the essence, inseparable from it? And he holds that God's attributes are identical to His essence, saying: 'Knowledge has no meaning except that the knower is knowing' and 'His being willing is identical to His will'—contrary to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, in whose speech it came that attributes are additional to the essence, necessary through another, possible in themselves. And the Ash'aris say: God has additional attributes, so He is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power, willing through will. And their strongest proof is their statement: If knowledge were the essence itself, and power were the essence itself, then knowledge would be power itself, and the concept of knowledge and power would be one. And we say to them: We see one person knows, wills, and has power. His knowledge appears to people independently, his will appears independently, and his power appears independently. His knowledge, will, and power are latent in his person, inseparable from him, such that if another person saw him face to face, he would not judge number between the person and his attributes, but rather judges seeing one person. And whenever an effect of an attribute appears, he is judged by it, without any statement of separation between essence and attribute. And the Mu'tazila—may God have mercy on them—say that God's attributes are in God's essence—and He is the same with His essence and attributes before the existence of the world, after it, and unto infinity. And they did not permit attributes additional to God's essence. Because God is eternal, and the attribute is eternal. So two eternals would follow in the world: God and the attribute, if it is said they are separate. But if it is said that the attribute is in the essence and they are one thing, then one eternal described with every perfection follows in the world, which is what must be said.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the core debate in Islamic theology regarding God's attributes (ṣifāt). Juwayni first frames the question: Are God's attributes identical to His essence (dhāt) or are they additional (zāʾida) yet inseparable? He notes that some hold the former view, arguing that 'knowledge' is just the state of being knowing, and will is identical to volition. This contrasts with Fakhr al-Din al-Rāzī, who held attributes are additional, necessary through God, but possible in themselves. Juwayni then outlines the Ashʿarī position: God has real attributes that are additional to His essence—He is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power, willing through will. Their strongest proof is a logical dilemma: if knowledge were identical to essence and power also identical to essence, then knowledge would be identical to power, collapsing the distinction between them—something absurd because we distinguish knowledge from power. The Ashʿarī then address an objection: we observe one person possessing distinct attributes (knowledge, will, power) that manifest independently, yet they are not separate from him; they are latent in his person. A viewer sees one person, not many. The same applies to God. Next, Juwayni presents the Muʿtazilī position (with the phrase 'may God have mercy on them' indicating respect despite disagreement). They hold that God's attributes are within His essence—He is the same before and after creation. They reject additional attributes because both God and attribute would be eternal, implying multiple eternals if the attribute is separate. Instead, they affirm one eternal described with all perfections. This summary captures the key soteriological and metaphysical stances of the two schools.

Key Terms
الأشاعرة
الصفات الزائدة
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe attributes are separate entities from God's essence.
Correction: Ash'aris hold attributes are additional in meaning (ma'na) but inseparable from essence; they are not independent beings.

Checkpoint
What is the strongest Ash'ari proof against the identity view?
A) If knowledge and power were both identical to essence, they would be identical to each other.
B) The Quran explicitly says attributes are additional.
C) Reason proves multiple eternals are impossible.
D) The Mu'tazila have no evidence.

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris affirm real, additional attributes through a logical proof that preserves God's unity and attribute distinctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God is 'Knowing, Powerful, Willing,' do you ever find yourself treating these as separate 'pieces' of God in your mind? How can you train your heart to see them as one with His essence, as the Ashʿarīs teach? This week, when you make dhikr, pause on one attribute and feel that it is not separate from God Himself.
?
The passage compares God's attributes to a person whose knowledge, will, and power are 'latent' and inseparable. In what area of your life do you habitually separate God's attributes from His essence—for example, trusting His power but forgetting His will? What concrete practice can you adopt to unify your perception of Him?
?
If a friend argued that believing in multiple attributes weakens tawḥīd, would you be able to explain the Ashʿarī position using the logical proof from this lesson? More importantly, does your own heart feel troubled by the idea of 'additional' attributes? Bring this fear to your prayer and ask God to grant you a sound understanding of His unity without stripping Him of His attributes.

intermediate
7 min
The Mu'tazila Position: Attributes Are Identical to Essence
Juwayni presents the Mu'tazila stance that attributes are identical to essence to avoid multiple eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view that God's attributes are identical to His essence, and their argument that separate attributes would imply multiple eternal beings.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila: God is one eternal; adding attributes creates multiple eternals.

Juwayni then presents the Mu'tazila position with a respectful prayer, showing their core concern: maintaining absolute divine unity. They argue that if attributes were additional and separate, they would be co-eternal with God, leading to polytheism. Instead, attributes are within the essence, making God one eternal being described by all perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the Mu'tazila view and their argument against additional attributes.
Notice the phrase 'may God have mercy on them' indicating respect despite disagreement.
2
Understand the logical problem
The Mu'tazila argue that if attributes are separate from essence, then both God and the attribute are eternal, implying two eternals. This violates tawhid.
Their solution: attributes are within the essence, so only one eternal exists.
3
Contrast with Ash'ari view
Compare this to the Ash'ari position: both reject separation, but Ash'aris say attributes are additional in meaning yet inseparable; Mu'tazila say they are identical.
This is a key point of divergence in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فى هذا بين كل من انتمى الى الاسلام . ولكن الجدال في ما معناه : هل صفات الله هي عين ذاته . أم صفاته زائدة على الذات غير منفكة عنها ؟ ويرى أن صفات الله هى عين ذاته يقول : لا معنى للعلم الا كون العالم عالما » - « وكونه مريدا عين ارادته » خلافا لفخر الدين الرازى الذى جاء في كلامه : أن الصفات زائدات على الذات ، واجبات بالغير ممكنات في حد ذاتها . والأشاعرة يقولون : ان الله صفات زائدة فهو عالم يعلم ، قادر بقدرة مريد بارادة . وأقوى دليل لهم قولهم : لو كان العلم نفس الذات ، والقدرة نفس الذات . لكان العلم نفس القدرة ، فكان المفهوم من العلم والقدرة واحدا . ونقول لهم : انا نرى الشخص الواحد يعلم ويريد ويقدر . ويظهر علمه للناس باستقلال ، وتظهر ارادته باستقلال ، وتظهر قدرته باستقلال . وعلمه وارادته وقدرته كامنة في شخصه غير منفكة عنه بحيث لو رآه شخص آخر مقابلة لم يحكم بالعدد بين الشخص وصفاته ، بل يحكم برؤية شخص واحد . وكلما تظهر أثر صفة حكم له بها دون ما قول بالفصل بين الذات والصفة . والمعتزلة - يرحمهم الله يقولون ان صفات الله فى ذات الله ، - وهو هو بذاته وصفاته من قبل وجود العالم ومن بعده والى ما لا نهاية . ولم يسرحوا بصفات زائدة على ذات الله . لأن الله قديم ، والصفة : قديمة . فيلزم فى العالم قديمان : الله والصفة اذا قيل بانفصالها . أما اذا قيل بالصفة فى الذات وهى والذات شيء واحد فانه يلزم في العالم : قديم واحد موصوف بكل كمال وهو ما يجب القول به
In this [work] is an explanation for everyone who belongs to Islam. But the debate is about its meaning: Are God's attributes identical to His essence? Or are His attributes additional to the essence, inseparable from it? And he holds that God's attributes are identical to His essence, saying: 'Knowledge has no meaning except that the knower is knowing' and 'His being willing is identical to His will'—contrary to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, in whose speech it came that attributes are additional to the essence, necessary through another, possible in themselves. And the Ash'aris say: God has additional attributes, so He is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power, willing through will. And their strongest proof is their statement: If knowledge were the essence itself, and power were the essence itself, then knowledge would be power itself, and the concept of knowledge and power would be one. And we say to them: We see one person knows, wills, and has power. His knowledge appears to people independently, his will appears independently, and his power appears independently. His knowledge, will, and power are latent in his person, inseparable from him, such that if another person saw him face to face, he would not judge number between the person and his attributes, but rather judges seeing one person. And whenever an effect of an attribute appears, he is judged by it, without any statement of separation between essence and attribute. And the Mu'tazila—may God have mercy on them—say that God's attributes are in God's essence—and He is the same with His essence and attributes before the existence of the world, after it, and unto infinity. And they did not permit attributes additional to God's essence. Because God is eternal, and the attribute is eternal. So two eternals would follow in the world: God and the attribute, if it is said they are separate. But if it is said that the attribute is in the essence and they are one thing, then one eternal described with every perfection follows in the world, which is what must be said.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the core debate in Islamic theology regarding God's attributes (ṣifāt). Juwayni first frames the question: Are God's attributes identical to His essence (dhāt) or are they additional (zāʾida) yet inseparable? He notes that some hold the former view, arguing that 'knowledge' is just the state of being knowing, and will is identical to volition. This contrasts with Fakhr al-Din al-Rāzī, who held attributes are additional, necessary through God, but possible in themselves. Juwayni then outlines the Ashʿarī position: God has real attributes that are additional to His essence—He is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power, willing through will. Their strongest proof is a logical dilemma: if knowledge were identical to essence and power also identical to essence, then knowledge would be identical to power, collapsing the distinction between them—something absurd because we distinguish knowledge from power. The Ashʿarī then address an objection: we observe one person possessing distinct attributes (knowledge, will, power) that manifest independently, yet they are not separate from him; they are latent in his person. A viewer sees one person, not many. The same applies to God. Next, Juwayni presents the Muʿtazilī position (with the phrase 'may God have mercy on them' indicating respect despite disagreement). They hold that God's attributes are within His essence—He is the same before and after creation. They reject additional attributes because both God and attribute would be eternal, implying multiple eternals if the attribute is separate. Instead, they affirm one eternal described with all perfections. This summary captures the key soteriological and metaphysical stances of the two schools.

Key Terms

القديم
الحدوث
الذات
الصفات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila deny that God has attributes entirely.
Correction: They affirm attributes but consider them identical to essence, not separate additions.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila reject attributes additional to essence?
A) Because it would imply multiple eternal beings.
B) Because the Quran says so.
C) Because Ash'aris are wrong.
D) Because attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila maintain divine unity by identifying attributes with essence, avoiding the problem of multiple eternals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God's oneness, do you ever unconsciously imagine separate 'parts' of God (like His power as something distinct from His mercy)? How does the Mu'tazila's fear of multiple eternals expose a subtle shirk in your own heart? Commit this week to reciting 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' while consciously affirming that Allah's attributes are not separate from Him.
?
This lesson shows sincere scholars disagreeing on a deep theological point. When you encounter a Muslim who holds a different view on a matter of aqeedah, do you seek to understand their reasoning, or do you instantly label them as misguided? Write down one area where you have been dismissive and ask Allah to grant you humility to learn.
?
The Mu'tazila were willing to risk logical difficulties to protect tawhid. Are you as zealous about God's oneness in your daily life—like in your trust in Him alone, or in avoiding showing off (riya)? Identify one practical situation where you can assert tawhid over your ego this week.

The Eternal Speech of God: Juwayni's Analogy
Juwayni defends the eternality of God's speech using the analogy of human planning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Juwayni uses the analogy of a person planning speech to demonstrate that God's attribute of speech is eternal and subsisting in His essence.

Core Idea
God's speech is like a plan in the mind—eternal and not created.

This passage presents one of the most intuitive analogies in Ash'ari theology. Juwayni invites us to reflect on our own experience of planning what to say. The meanings we intend are present in our minds before we utter a single word. He then applies this to God, arguing that His speech—the meanings He will convey to creation—subsists eternally in His essence, just as His power subsists without beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Juwayni passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a human analogy followed by its divine application.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow from human to divine.
2
Identify the human analogy
Juwayni describes a person who resolves to speak to a friend after a month. The meanings of that future speech are already present in his soul. Find this and consider why it matters.
The word 'نفس' (nafs) here means soul or self, not breath.
3
Apply the analogy to God
Juwayni extends the analogy to God: just as the human planner has meanings in his soul, God in His pre-eternity has the meanings of what He will say to His servants. Note how Juwayni emphasises God's freedom from forgetfulness.
This step is the core of the argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Juwayni concludes that God's speech subsisting in Himself is like His eternal power—neither begins nor ceases. Reflect on how this defends the Ash'ari view of an eternal Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكد الجويني على مذهبه في الصفات وهو يتحدث في كلام الله تعالى فقال : ( إن من يعزم على مفاوضة صاحب له بعد شهر ، فالمعاني التي سيوردها عند جريان الجواز يجدها بأعيانها قائمة في نفسه ثم اذا حان الوقت أداها فأنهاها . والعالم بأنه سيكلم فلانا لا تخلو فسه عن وجود ثبوت ذلك الكلام على تقدير وجوده في العبارات من حين المفاوضة تبلغ تلك المعانى . والرب فى أزله كان عالما بأنه يتعبد عبادة اذا وجدوا وهو العالم المقدس عن أن يسهو أو يهفو فلا يخلى وجوده الأزلى عن معنى ما سيصل الى العباد اذا وجدوا : وسبيل ذلك الكلام القائم بنفسه . كسبيل قدرته القديمة ولم تزل » • يريد أن يقول : كما أن الانسان لو أراد أن يحدث صاحبا له بعد شهر فى أمر ما ، يرتب كلاما في نفسه ليقوله لصاحبه بعد شهر . فاذا جاء الشهر نطق بالكلام الذى كان قد رتبه فى نفسه ذلك . وهذا الانسان قد أراد وقد أوجد معانى فى نفسه ثم نطق بهذه المعاني . ومع أنه وصفاته شيء واحد من قبل . كذلك الله وجل - والله المثل الأعلى - صفاته من قبل وما تزال الكلام القائم بنفسه كسبيل قدرته القديمة . ولم تزل . ولما تحدث في « كلام الله » لم يمش على تعبيره « ولم يزل » ذلك أنه أثبت الله كلاما لم ينفصل عنه في قوله « كلام الله الأزلى لا يفارق الذات ولا يزايلها » وإن كان يقصد بهذه العبارة « النظم الدال على المعنى المتحقق لفظا ومعنى فى علم الله أزلا ( كترتيب الانسان كلام في نفسه ليؤديه الى صاحبه بعد شهر فلا اعتراض عليه . لأنه يكون كارادته و قدرته من قبل أن يحدث الله الذي أراده . فاذا أحدث الذي أراده فان الذى حدث يكون حادثا • كارادته خلق العالم أزلا فانه لما خلق العالم صار العالم منفصلا بالارادة ومخلوقا حادثا . كذلك لو تكلم فقال كن » مثلا . فان « كن » تعتبر فى المعنى كخلق العالم بعد أن كان فكرة . وكما تشاهد قدرة الله لا تزال ، كذلك كلامه لا يزال أو بتعبير الجويني نفسه « ولم يزل » لقد ظهرت قدرات كثيرة الله على طول الزمان .
And Juwayni emphasized his school regarding attributes while speaking about the speech of God Most High, saying: 'Indeed, whoever resolves to converse with a companion after a month, the meanings he will deliver at the time of meeting he finds in their very selves subsisting in his soul; then when the time comes, he performs them and completes them. And the one who knows that he will speak to so-and-so is not devoid in his soul of the existence of the affirmation of that speech, given its existence in expressions from the time of the conversation that delivers those meanings. And the Lord in His pre-eternity was knowing that He would worship servants when they exist, and He is the Knower sanctified from forgetfulness or oversight, so His pre-eternal existence is not devoid of the meaning of what will reach the servants when they exist. And the way of that speech subsisting in itself is like the way of His eternal power, which has not ceased.' He intends to say: Just as a person, if he wants to speak to a companion after a month concerning a matter, arranges speech in himself to say to his companion after a month, so when the month comes, he utters the speech that he had arranged in himself. And that person has willed and has originated meanings in himself, then uttered those meanings, while he and his attributes are one thing from before. Likewise God Almighty—and God has the highest example—His attributes from before and His speech subsisting in itself continues, like the way of His eternal power, which has not ceased. And when he spoke about 'the speech of God,' he did not proceed with his expression 'has not ceased,' that he affirmed that God has speech that does not separate from Him in his statement 'the eternal speech of God does not leave the essence nor depart from it.' And if he meant by this expression 'the order indicating the meaning realized verbally and conceptually in God's knowledge eternally,' as a person arranges speech in himself to deliver to his companion after a month, then there is no objection to it. Because it is like His will and His power from before He brings about what He willed. So when He brings about what He willed, then what occurs is originated. Like His will creating the world eternally, for when He created the world, the world became separate by the will and a created originated thing. Likewise if He spoke and said 'Be' for example, then 'Be' is considered in meaning like the creation of the world after it was an idea. And just as you see God's power does not cease, likewise His speech does not cease; or in Juwayni's own expression, 'has not ceased.' Many powers of God have appeared over the length of time.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the Ashʿarī view that God's attribute of speech (kalām Allāh) is eternal and subsisting in His essence. Juwayni begins with an analogy: a person who plans to converse with a friend after a month already has the meanings of that speech present in his mind, and when the time comes, he delivers them. Similarly, the knower is not devoid of that intended speech in his soul. God, in His pre-eternity, knew He would address His servants when they exist, and He is free from forgetfulness, so His eternal existence includes the meaning of what will reach them. Juwayni then explicitly states that this speech subsisting in itself (al-kalām al-qāʾim bi-nafsih) is like His eternal power (al-qudra al-qadīma) which has never ceased (lam tazal). He rephrases the analogy: a human arranges speech in himself and then utters it; the human and his attributes are one. Likewise, God's attributes are eternal, and His speech subsists in His essence. Juwayni then addresses a possible objection to the phrase 'has not ceased' (lam yazal). He clarifies that he affirms a speech that never separates from the essence, as in his statement 'the eternal speech of God does not leave the essence nor depart from it.' He interprets this as referring to the ordered expression (al-naẓm) indicating the meaning realized linguistically and conceptually in God's knowledge eternally. The example of a person arranging speech in himself is given to show that there is no objection to this; it is like God's will and power preceding creation. When God creates, the created thing is originated (ḥādith). Similarly, if God says 'Be' (kun), it is like creation after it was an idea. God's power and speech never cease, as evidenced by the continuous appearance of His powers throughout time.

Key Terms

أزلي
قائم بالذات
لم يزل
قدرة
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That believing God's speech is eternal implies it is identical to created sounds or letters.
Correction: Juwayni clarifies that God's speech is a meaning subsisting in His essence, not a temporal event of sound or script. The human analogy shows that speech as a mental meaning precedes utterance, just as God's speech is eternal.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Juwayni use to explain the eternality of God's speech?
A) A person planning to speak to a friend after a month.
B) The creation of the world from nothing.
C) The comparison of God's speech to human writing.
D) The impossibility of multiple eternals.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, not a created event; it is like a plan in the mind that never ceases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Juwayni argues that God's speech is not a created event but an eternal attribute. When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly feel you are uttering words that have always existed in God's essence? How does this truth affect your attention and reverence during recitation? What one change can you make today to approach the Qur'an with the awe it deserves?
?
If speech as a meaning in the soul can be present before utterance, then God's speech is not dependent on creation. How does this challenge any assumption you may have that God's actions are reactive to human affairs? In what area of your life do you need to trust that God's word to you—through revelation—was already perfectly planned before you existed?
?
The analogy compares human planning to God's eternal speech. But human plans often change or fail. God's speech never changes. When you face uncertainty or trial, do you seek comfort in God's unchanging words, or do you rely on your own fluctuating thoughts? What step can you take this week to anchor your heart more firmly in His eternal speech?
