Objection: Perpetual Food vs. Annihilation
Juwayni presents an objection based on the contradiction between perpetual food and universal perishing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if Paradise were created before Judgment Day, its food would perish, contradicting its eternal nature.

Core Idea
If Paradise were created, its food would perish, contradicting its perpetual description.

Opponents raise a logical objection: the Qur'an describes Paradise as having perpetual food, yet everything except Allah perishes. If Paradise were created, its food would be subject to perishing. How can this contradiction be resolved? Juwayni implies their understanding is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage where opponents cite two verses: one describing perpetual food, the other stating everything perishes.
Note the specific verses: 13:35 and 28:88.
2
Understand the contradiction
The opponents argue that perpetual food cannot coexist with universal perishing if Paradise is created. Identify the logical chain.
3
Consider Juwayni's implicit response
Juwayni does not give a direct answer here, but he uses the word 'فهمهم' (their understanding) suggesting the objection is based on a flawed interpretation. Reflect on possible responses.
One possible response is that perishing applies to the worldly creation, not the Hereafter.

Source Text

Objection: Expanse of Paradise and Interpenetration
Juwayni presents an objection based on the verse about expanse and the impossibility of interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Paradise's expanse being the heavens and earth implies it cannot be created before the annihilation of the universe, and Juwayni's suggestion that the expression is metaphorical.

Core Idea
Paradise's expanse equals heavens and earth, so it must await their annihilation to avoid interpenetration.

Opponents adduce a verse about Paradise's expanse being the heavens and earth. They argue that if Paradise were created now, it would interpenetrate the heavens and earth, which is impossible. Therefore, Paradise must be created after the heavens and earth perish. Juwayni offers a metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and objection
Read the verse 'Its expanse is the heavens and the earth' and the opponents' reasoning.
They claim that if Paradise existed, it would occupy the same space as the heavens and earth.
2
Understand the argument about interpenetration
The impossibility of interpenetration of bodies (تداخل الأجسام) is a physical principle. Grasp why this is a challenge.
3
Consider Juwayni's metaphorical interpretation
Juwayni suggests the expression might be a metaphor for vastness, not literal dimensions. Reflect on how this resolves the objection.
Metaphorical readings are common in theological debates.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The opponents used rational argument to deny the existence of Paradise before the afterlife. Have you ever used reason to doubt a promise of Allah? What does this lesson teach you about balancing faith and intellect?
?
If Paradise is described as vast beyond imagination (metaphorically), does that inspire more awe in you than a literal measurement? Or do you find yourself wanting concrete details? How can you cultivate a deeper longing for Paradise based on this understanding?

Qur'anic Proofs Equating Kufr and Fisq (1)
Lists five Qur'anic proofs equating disbelief and transgression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how five Qur'anic verses directly or indirectly equate disbelief (kufr) with transgression (fisq).

Core Idea
The Qur'an repeatedly calls disbelievers transgressors.

Imam Juwayni continues his proof that kufr and fisq are the same by citing five Qur'anic verses. Each verse either directly states that disbelievers are transgressors or associates a trait of transgression with disbelief. This lesson covers the ninth through thirteenth proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how each proof is introduced with a number.
The passage uses a compressed style; the author only quotes key parts of each verse.
2
Identify the connection in each verse
For each of the five proofs, determine how the verse links disbelief (kufr) with transgression (fisq). Is it explicit, or does it associate a common trait?
3
Understand the logical inference in the ninth proof
The ninth proof argues that the verse 'whoever disbelieves... are the transgressors' implies the predicate from the subject. This means the disbeliever is identified as a transgressor.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: If the Qur'an repeatedly uses 'fasiq' for disbelievers, what does that say about the view that fisq is a separate category between faith and disbelief?
This connects to the broader theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the ninth: 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the transgressors' (An-Nur:55) for it implies the predicate from the subject, i.e., the disbeliever is a transgressor. And the tenth: His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the mercy of Allah except the disbelieving people' (Yusuf:87) and the transgressor despairs of mercy. And the eleventh: His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, whoever You cause to enter the Fire, You have disgraced him' (Al-Imran:192) with His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' (An-Nahl:27). And the twelfth: His saying, exalted: 'As for he who is given his record in his left hand' until His saying: 'Indeed, he did not believe in Allah, the Most Great' (Al-Haqqah:25-33). And the thirteenth: His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers' (Al-A'raf:44). And the fourteenth: His saying, exalted: 'And as for those who transgressed, their refuge is the Fire' (As-Sajdah:20). And the fifteenth: His saying, exalted: 'They ask each other about the criminals. What caused you to enter Saqar?' until His saying: 'And we used to deny the Day of Recompense' (Al-Muddaththir:40-46). And the sixteenth: His saying, exalted: 'And those who disbelieved will be driven' until His saying: 'And those who feared Allah will be led' (Az-Zumar:71-73). And the seventeenth: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever deliberately abandons prayer has disbelieved' and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies without having performed Hajj, let him die as a Jew if he wishes or as a Christian if he wishes.' And the eighteenth: The friendship of Allah and His enmity are two opposites, so there is no intermediate between them, and the friendship of Allah is faith, so His enmity is disbelief.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of scriptural proofs demonstrating that disbelief and transgression (fisq) are not separate categories but rather one and the same. The author lists ten proofs (numbered ninth through eighteenth, continuing from a previous list) to establish that the term 'fasiq' (transgressor) applies to the disbeliever, or that disbelief necessarily entails fisq. Each proof is either a Qur'anic verse or a hadith. The method is to show that in the texts, kufr and fisq are linked either explicitly (e.g., 'then those are the transgressors' applied to disbelievers) or implicitly (e.g., the transgressor despairs of mercy, which is a trait of disbelievers). The proofs range from direct identification (ninth: 'whoever disbelieves... are the transgressors') to association (eleventh: disgrace is for disbelievers and the transgressor is disgraced) to logical inference (eighteenth: friendship and enmity are opposites, and since friendship is faith, enmity is disbelief, implying no middle ground). The hadith about abandoning prayer and dying without Hajj are used to equate certain acts of transgression with disbelief. The structure is a straightforward list, each item introduced by its number and the textual evidence.

Correction: The Qur'an directly calls disbelievers 'fasiqun' (transgressors), showing that kufr itself is a form of fisq, not a separate category.

Key Takeaway

Qur'anic and Hadith Proofs Equating Kufr and Fisq (2)
Lists five more proofs: two verses, two hadiths, and a rational inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how five more proofs (including hadiths and a logical inference) establish that disbelief and transgression are identical.

Core Idea
Scripture and reason together erase any gap between kufr and fisq.

Imam Juwayni continues his enumeration with the fourteenth through eighteenth proofs. These include two more Qur'anic verses, two hadiths, and a logical argument based on the opposition between Allah's friendship and enmity. This lesson completes the list and reinforces the doctrine that there is no middle category between faith and disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the proofs become more varied: two verses, then two hadiths, then a rational argument.
2
Analyze the fourteenth and fifteenth proofs
The fourteenth proof mentions that the refuge of those who 'fasaqu' is the Fire. The fifteenth proof links entering Saqar (Hell) with denying the Day of Judgment, a form of disbelief.
These show that fisq leads to the same punishment as kufr.
3
Examine the sixteenth proof
This proof contrasts the gathering of disbelievers and the righteous, implying that transgression is a trait of disbelievers.
4
Evaluate the hadith proofs (seventeenth)
The hadiths about abandoning prayer and dying without Hajj equate specific acts of transgression with disbelief. Consider what this means for the definition of faith.
These hadiths are often cited in theological debates about the boundaries of faith.
5
Understand the logical proof (eighteenth)
The author argues that Allah's friendship (wilaya) and enmity ('adawa) are opposites with no intermediate. Since wilaya is faith, enmity must be disbelief. Therefore, there is no middle ground like fisq.
This is a rational argument complementing the scriptural proofs.

Juwayni's Reinterpretation of 'Al-Abad'
Juwayni refutes the view that sinful Muslims remain in Hell forever by reinterpreting 'al-abad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Juwayni reinterprets the term 'al-abad' to argue that sinful Muslims will not remain in Hell forever but will eventually enter Paradise.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment for sinful Muslims is avoided by redefining 'al-abad' as a limited period.

This lesson presents a key theological argument from Imam al-Juwayni concerning the fate of sinful Muslims in the afterlife. He challenges the apparent meaning of 'al-abad' (eternity) in the Quran, suggesting it can carry a metaphorical sense of a finite duration. By doing so, he refutes those who claim that Muslim sinners will suffer indefinitely in Hell. The passage also includes the opposing view and a critical note about the origin of this reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
First, read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms 'خلود', 'أبد', and 'عصاة'. Try to identify the different claims being made.
The text may have OCR issues; focus on the main arguments.
2
Identify Juwayni's refutation
Look for how Juwayni reinterprets 'al-abad'. He says it does not mean endless eternity but a period with an end. Understand the consequence: sinful Muslims will eventually leave Hell.
3
Understand the opposing view
The passage also presents the view of the scholars Juwayni refutes. They believe sins become good deeds or that judgment depends on scales, leading to eternal outcome in Paradise or Fire. Notice the different eschatology.
4
Consider the claim about Jewish origin
The text includes a controversial statement that the limited interpretation of 'al-abad' came from Jewish tradition. Think about why Juwayni might reject this interpretation for Muslims.
This may be a polemical point.
5
Reflect on the missing proofs
The passage ends by mentioning two proofs, but they are not given. Consider what kinds of proofs Juwayni might have used (e.g., Quranic verses, logical arguments). This shows the fragmentary nature of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الجوينى على العلماء القائلين بخلود عصاة المسلمين في النار فشرح لهم لفظ ( الأبد ( على أنه لا يعنى ظاهر اللفظ وهو الأبد بما لا نهاية. له . بل يعنى المجاز وهو .. أبد له نهاية . أى يعذبون في النار مدة ثم يخرجون من النار الى الجنة .
تاب
قال هؤلاء العلماء الذين رد عليهم الجوينى : ان المسلم العاصى اذا ثم مات فان ذنوبه تبدل حسنات . ويخلد في الجنة ، واذا مات ولم .. تب ستوضع له الموازين فإن ثقلت الموازين دخل الجنة خالدا فيها ، وان خفت الموازين دخل النار خالدا فيها فى دركة على قدر عصيانه
والفكرة القاتلة بأن لفظ « الأبد » يعنى أبدا له نهاية دخلت في الإسلام من دين بنى اسرائیل . فعندهم قرائن تنهى الأبد . أما نحن المسلمين فلا قرائن عندنا . واستدل على ذلك بدليلين :
And Juwayni refuted the scholars who say that sinful Muslims abide eternally in the Fire, and he explained to them the word 'al-abad' (eternity) that it does not mean the apparent meaning, which is eternity without end, but rather the metaphorical meaning, which is an eternity with an end, i.e., they will be punished in the Fire for a period then emerge from the Fire to Paradise. 
Continued.
Those scholars whom Juwayni refuted said: The sinful Muslim, when he then dies, his sins will be changed into good deeds, and he will abide eternally in Paradise. And if he dies and ... the scales will be set up for him. If his scales are heavy, he enters Paradise, abiding eternally therein. If his scales are light, he enters the Fire, abiding eternally therein, in a degree corresponding to his disobedience. 
And the deadly idea that the word 'al-abad' means an eternity with an end entered Islam from the religion of the Children of Israel. For they have indicators that terminate eternity. As for us Muslims, we have no such indicators. And he adduced two proofs:
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage records Juwayni's refutation of a group of scholars who held that sinful Muslims would be punished in Hell forever (khulud). Juwayni reinterprets the word 'al-abad' (eternity) in the relevant texts, arguing that it does not carry its literal meaning of endless duration but rather a metaphorical sense: an 'eternity' that has an end. He explains that this means they will be punished in the Fire for a limited period and then brought out to Paradise. The text then (perhaps as a separate section or continuation) presents the view of the scholars Juwayni refuted: they held a complex eschatology where the sinful Muslim's sins are transformed into good deeds at death, and if not, they are subject to the scales of judgment leading either to eternal Paradise or eternal Fire in a degree matching their sin. The passage then makes a critical remark that the 'deadly idea' that 'al-abad' can mean a limited period entered Islam from Jewish sources, because the Jews have indicators (qara'in) that can terminate an eternal decree, whereas Muslims have no such indicators. It ends by stating that Juwayni adduced two proofs, but those proofs are not included in the given text. The structure is: first Juwayni's refutation, then the opposing view, then a polemical note on the origin of the interpretation, likely pointing out a heretical innovation.

Correction: Juwayni argues that in this context, 'al-abad' can be interpreted metaphorically as a period that does have an end, allowing for the eventual salvation of sinful Muslims.

Juwayni's Two Interpretations of Divine Forgiveness
Juwayni interprets the Qur'anic verse on divine forgiveness and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's two possible interpretations of the phrase 'for whom He wills' in Quran 4:48.

Core Idea
Divine forgiveness is both an act of will and a response to repentance.

This lesson examines Imam Juwayni's nuanced interpretation of a key Qur'anic verse on forgiveness. Juwayni offers two readings: one emphasizing God's absolute will, and another that ties forgiveness to human repentance. Understanding this flexibility is essential for grasping the interplay of divine will and human agency in Ash'ari thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note how Juwayni presents two distinct interpretations.
The verse itself is quoted; focus on Juwayni's explanation.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Juwayni first says 'whom Allah wills forgiveness for' (divine will), then offers an alternative: 'whoever wills forgiveness for himself, so he repents, then Allah forgives him' (human repentance).
Notice the shift in agency—from God to the human.
3
Reflect on theological significance
Ask yourself why Juwayni includes both interpretations. How does this reflect Ash'ari theology on divine will and human responsibility?
This connects to broader debates on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكى يؤكد الجوينى رأيه . فسر قوله تعالى : ( ان الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء ) بقوله أى من يشاء الله له الغفران . ومن الممكن أن تفسر : لمن يشاء لنفسه مغفرة فيتوب فيغفر الله له
And in order to confirm his opinion, he interpreted the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48) by his saying: i.e., whom Allah wills forgiveness for. And it is possible to interpret it as: for whoever wills forgiveness for himself, so he repents, then Allah forgives him.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents Juwayni's interpretation of the Qur'anic verse on divine forgiveness (4:48). The author reports that Juwayni, to support his view, explains the phrase 'for whom He wills' in two possible ways. The first interpretation is that the forgiveness is solely dependent on God's will—He forgives whomever He chooses. The second interpretation shifts the agency to the human: it means 'for whoever wills forgiveness for himself by repenting, then Allah forgives him.' Juwayni thus allows for both a predestinarian and a conditional understanding of the verse, showing flexibility in exegesis.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الغفران
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Juwayni only advocates for unconditional predestination in interpreting this verse.
Correction: In fact, Juwayni presents both an unconditional (divine will) and a conditional (repentance) interpretation, showing a balanced approach.

Checkpoint
According to Juwayni's interpretation of 4:48, what are the two possible meanings of 'for whom He wills'?
A) Whom Allah wills to forgive, or whoever wills forgiveness for himself through repentance.
B) Only those who never sin, or only the prophets.
C) Those who ask for forgiveness, or those who perform good deeds.
D) Whom Allah wills to punish, or those who disbelieve.

Key Takeaway
Juwayni's interpretation shows that divine forgiveness is both a matter of God's will and open to human repentance, reflecting Ash'ari theological flexibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Juwayni presents two interpretations: one where forgiveness depends solely on God's will, and another where it is conditional on your repentance. Which interpretation do you actually live by when you sin? Do you postpone repentance, assuming God's will might forgive you anyway? Or do you rush to repent, knowing that your repentance is the channel through which His forgiveness flows? Be honest: what does your pattern of seeking forgiveness reveal about your real belief?
?
If you truly believed that Allah forgives 'whom He wills' and that He wills forgiveness for those who repent, how would that change the way you approach your worst sin tonight? Not in general—specifically, what step of repentance will you take before you sleep?
?
Juwayni allowed two meanings for the same Qur'anic phrase. Are you as flexible in your understanding of God's mercy? Do you judge others' chances of forgiveness based on your own narrow interpretation, forgetting that God's will and their repentance are both at play? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about someone you have written off?
